--- VISUAL TEXT (VLM) ---
Hardest 7 Days of My Life:
(Here's The Deepest Truths About the Mind, Life, and Reality)
I meditated 15 hours a day for 7 days.
No talking. No phone. Here's what
happens when you do.


DAY 1: WE SUFFER TWICE - FIRST IN THE
MIND, THEN IN REALITY
Buddhists have a word for mental suffering: dukkha, the
pain we create with our own thoughts.
On the first day of silence, I discovered how real this is.
I was doing nothing, yet my mind became a battlefield of
regrets and what-ifs, worrying about things that weren't
even happening.
Lesson: 90% of suffering
is just mental rehearsal.
The past only exists if
you keep feeding it.
We'll all ase


DAY 2: YOUR MIND IS NOT YOU - IT'S JUST
NOISE
By the second day, my mind wasn't just talking
it was
screaming.
One moment I was reliving old memories; the next, I was
imagining worst-case scenarios.
I even found myself craving random things - food, music, any
distraction to avoid the stillness.
Then I realized something: my mind is like a noise machine.
constantly generating thoughts even when there's no real reason.
It will chatter about anything and everything, but that chatter isn't
me - it's just mental noise.
Lesson: Your thoughts are
not reality: they're just
noise. If you stop
identifying with them, they
lose their power over you.


DAY 3: CRAVING IS AN ENDLESS HUNGER
It's easy to think you're content-until everything is taken
away.
With no phone, no distractions, no comfort food, and no
validation from others, my mind suddenly became
desperate for something, anything to fill the emptiness.
I realized that most of my unrest wasn't due to actual pain
or problems, but due to craving.
The mind is always grasping for the next thing to hold onto,
never satisfied with the present moment.
-
The Great Dreaming Sagad Lotus Stupa
Lesson: Most of our suffering isn't from
pain - it's from craving. We're always
needing more and never satisfied. When
you stop chasing, you realize you already
have everything you need.


DAY 4: SILENCE IS THE
HARDEST MIRROR TO
FACE
People say they love peace, but most
have never truly sat in it.
Real stillness doesn't just relax you
it exposes you.
In the silence, there were no
distractions from my emotions, no
escaping my thoughts, and no
avoiding the truths about my life.
Everything I'd been running from was
suddenly right in front of me.
Lesson: Stillness is
terrifying because it forces
you to meet yourself. And
most people are running
from that encounter.


DAY 5: THE BODY KNOWS WHAT
THE MIND IGNORES
By day five, I noticed a pattern between my mind
and body.
Whenever I had an uncomfortable thought, my
jaw would clench.
When an old emotional wound surfaced, my.
stomach tightened.
If I started worrying about the future, my breath
turned shallow.
It became clear that my body was expressing the
stress and emotions that my mind usually tries
to bury or ignore.
The signals were always there; I just hadn't been
quiet enough to notice them before.
Lesson: Your body speaks what
your mind suppresses. If you want
to heal, stop overthinking and
start feeling.


DAY 6: THERE IS NO SEPARATION - EVERYTHING IS ONE
At a certain point in deep silence, something shifts.
The familiar illusion of "me" versus "you" begins to dissolve.
I watched birds in the trees and felt them as part of me.
1 walked barefoot on the ground and felt the Earth breathing
beneath my feet.
Il looked up at the sky and realized I am as much a part of it as
it is of me
In that state, the boundaries between self and other, between
person and nature, faded away.
There was only an overwhelming sense of connection to
everything around me
Lesson: We spend our lives acting like we're
separate from nature, from each other, from
everything. But there is no separation - only a
connection we've been conditioned to forget.


DAY 7: THERE IS NO "YOU" - ONLY
AWARENESS EXPERIENCING ITSELF
By the last day, something inside me cracked open.
I had spent my entire life believing I was this person - a name,
a body, a story shaped by my past.
But in true silence, even that identity faded away.
What was left when I wasn't clinging to my "self?"
Nothing but pure presence, just awareness watching
everything. I was the observer behind the thoughts, not the
thinker.
"I was the presence behind the experiences,not the persona
they were happening to.
Lesson: You are not a name. not a story, not even a
body. You are the one watching it all unfold - the
observer behind the experience, the awareness behind
the thoughts. And once you see that, fear dies.


WHEN BELIEF BECOMES A BARRIER TO TRUTH
Religion, philosophy, ideology - they all begin with the
intention to help us find meaning.
But somewhere along the way, they often do the opposite.
They create labels and divisions
They tell us what to believe, who is "right" and who is
"wrong."
In that process, we forget the most important insight:
reality doesn't need a belief system.
It just is.
The more we cling to pre-packaged ideas of truth, the
more removed we become from experiencing reality
directly.
Lesson: The more you seek truth outside
yourself the further you drift from it. The
moment you let go of all ideas, doctrines,
and labels, you stop believing and start
experiencing. After all, the deepest wisdom
doesn't come from a book, a teacher, or a
creed - it comes from silence.


TRUTH IS NOT UNIVERSAL-IT'S PERSONAL
We love to talk about "the truth."
The ultimate answer.
The one way.
-
The final understanding.
But here's the reality: Truth isn't a
fact-it's a perspective.
Every person sees the world through
the lens of their experiences, beliefs,
and conditioning.
What's true for me may not be true
for you.
And that's okay.
My truth is just that-mine.
VISIT LMBINI YEAR 2012
Yours is yours.
 And neither has to be wrong for both to exist. The biggest illusion? Thinking that we all have to agree
on the same version of truth.
Because the deeper you go, the more you realize:
Truth isn't something you find -it's something you live.

Post Link: https://www.instagram.com/p/DTBRJFTDL4C/
Type: Reel
Caption: Letting go is not enough.
You are still giving away your freedom.

You tell yourself you’re detached.You say you’ve learned not to cling. But look closely at how you actually live. Notice how your body tightens when outcomes feel uncertain. How the mind becomes restless the moment something important slips out of control. How effort turns anxious when results stop obeying you.

This isn’t because you want too much. And it isn’t because you’re too involved in the world. It’s because you began relating to life as something you must possess in order to feel safe.

This is exactly what the Isha Upanishad was pointing at when it said that everything here is pervaded by the Divine and therefore must be lived in, not owned.

Action was never the problem.
Ownership was.

That’s why the same text tells you to live fully, to act, to engage, even for a hundred years, but without letting possession take root inside you.

The moment you say “this is mine” and mean it internally, something shifts. Effort becomes heavy. Ambition becomes brittle. Even good intentions start to exhaust you.

Greek mythology told the same story through King Midas. He didn’t suffer because he loved gold. He suffered because everything he touched had to belong to him.

Aristotle made a similar point. He argued that virtue isn’t withdrawal from life, but right participation in it.

When life is something you’re part of rather than something you own, action changes. It becomes lighter. Less desperate. More precise.

You can build, lead, earn, create, and love deeply. What matters is whether your sense of self depends on controlling outcomes. A person with clear vision can live in the middle of chaos and remain free. A person with confused vision can renounce everything and still feel trapped.

Freedom doesn’t come from doing less.
It comes from gripping less.

You don’t escape life to become free.
You enter it without being owned by it.

#personaldevelopment #sanatandharma #hindu #spirituality #shiva
--------------------
--- AUDIO TRANSCRIPT ---
There is a line from the Isha Upanishad that overturns how most people think spirituality works. Everything here is pervaded by the Divine. Sit with that for a second. If everything is sacred, then where exactly does spiritual life and unworldly life begin? Because the Upanishads never made that distinction, they never made that separation. We did. Somewhere along the way, we started treating life like a compromise, as if spiritual depth requires withdrawal, as if clarity demands distance, as if involvement in material workings automatically corrupts you. The Isha Upanishad does not agree. It says a few lines later, something even more unsettling. Live fully, act, engage in the world even for a hundred years, which raises the real question. If everything is sacred and action is not the problem, then what is actually binding us? If you're thinking the answer is effort, ambition or participation, you're wrong. It is ownership. The Upanishads do not warn you against enjoying life. They warn you against gripping it too tightly. The moment that you say this is mine, your nervous system changes. A strange kind of fear and defensiveness starts creeping in. But when you relate to life as something you're a part of and not something that you possess, your action becomes lighter and less desperate. And this idea isn't just unique to Sanat and Dharm, by the way. In Greek mythology, King Midas didn't suffer because he loved gold. He suffered because he couldn't stop claiming it. Everything he touched had to belong to him, and that is what destroyed him. Aristotle made a similar point in philosophy. He argued that virtue isn't withdrawal from life but right participation in it. Excess binds you, deficiency weakens you, and clarity sits right in the middle of the two. Even in Chinese thought, Lao Tzu said something quietly radical. The sage acts without clinging to the results. Things get done but nothing is grasped. So spirituality was never about leaving the world behind. It was about entering it without being owned by it as I've probably said too many times by now. You can build, lead, earn. You can love deeply. But what matters is whether your sense of self depends on controlling outcomes. Because a person with a clear vision can live in the middle of chaos and remain free, and a person with confused vision can renounce everything and still be trapped. The Upanishads did not glorify escape. They refined perception. When you see the world correctly, action stops being a burden. It becomes a form of alignment. You're no longer trying to possess life, you're participating in it.

Post Link: https://www.instagram.com/p/DSQTNjqjEDF/
Type: Reel
Caption: What is the point of all this?

At some point in life, almost everyone runs into the same invisible wall. You work. You try. You put in effort. You make choices. And yet reality keeps pushing back. In this quiet, repetitive, strangely precise way that makes you stop and think.

Why effort, consequence, repetition?
Why does life feel less like a gift and more like a lesson you didn’t consciously sign up for? 
Why were you even created?
Why’s there something as opposed to nothing?

This is where Sanatana Dharma becomes very different from how most people imagine faith.

It doesn’t rush to comfort you. It doesn’t immediately tell you everything is “for the best.” And it definitely doesn’t shut the question down.

One of the oldest hymns we have, the Rig Veda itself, openly wonders how existence even began and then admits something rare that, maybe no one really knows. Not even the highest observer.

That tone matters. It tells you this tradition isn’t obsessed with certainty. It’s obsessed with honesty.

Here, karma isn’t framed as punishment.
It isn’t reward either.
It’s closer to feedback.

If consciousness enters experience, action becomes unavoidable. And once there is action, consequence follows, because cause and effect are woven into reality itself.

Sanatana Dharma doesn’t say the world exists so you can suffer. It says suffering begins when consciousness forgets what it is while moving through form.

So slowly, almost without you noticing, the question changes.

You stop asking, “Why did this happen to me?”
And start asking something far more uncomfortable.

“What is this experience trying to teach consciousness about itself?”

If you’ve ever felt like life keeps circling the same lessons, the same friction, the same inner resistance, this reel isn’t here to hand you answers.

It’s here to help you look at the question properly.

And sometimes, that’s what actually changes everything.

#personaldevelopment #hindu #sanatandharma #spirituality #shiva #hinduism
--------------------
--- AUDIO TRANSCRIPT ---
There is a question almost everyone asks at some point. Why are we here at all? Why is there something instead of nothing? Why do we have to go through karmas, suffering, effort and consequence? If the self is already Brahman, why this whole drama? Why this whole mess? What is interesting is that Sanatana Dharma doesn't rush to answer this the way other religions usually do. It doesn't start with rigid commandments, but it starts with a question. The Rig Veda in the Nasadiyya Sukta openly asks how existence even began and then says something Who really knows? Perhaps even the one who watches from the highest heaven does not know. And that alone tells you something important. Sanatana Dharma is not obsessed with giving you certainty, but it is obsessed with honesty. Some Upanishads say that the one will to become many. Others suggest that manifestation is a natural overflow and not a planned project. Advait Vedant goes further and says that from the highest level, nothing was ever really created There is only Brahman appearing as multiplicity. So already you see the pattern. There isn't one rigid answer because the question itself changes depending on the level you're looking from. Now if we talk about karma, Sanatana Dharma doesn't present karma as a punishment system. It is not cosmic reward and punishment that is feedback. If consciousness enters experience, action becomes unavoidable. And where there is action, consequence becomes unavoidable because cause and effect is built into reality itself. Therefore, karma exists because experience exists. If there were no karma, there would be no learning, just awareness trapped in repetition without understanding itself. And that is why many texts say that liberation doesn't come from escaping the world, but from seeing through it. From acting, observing, learning and eventually recognizing the pattern. And here's the part that people miss. Sanatana Dharma doesn't say that the world exists so you can suffer. It says suffering happens because of ignorance, because consciousness forgets its own nature while moving through form. The law of karma therefore is not the enemy, but ignorance is. Karma is what slowly teaches consciousness about itself. Through contrast, through effort, through consequence. And the question, why is there something instead of nothing is answered very differently here. Sanatana Dharma doesn't assume nothing was ever the default. Being is, existence is. Manifestation comes and goes in cycles like breath, creation, dissolution and creation again. So the question becomes not, why did God create this, but why does consciousness experience itself this way? And the most honest answer Hindu thought gives is this, because experience allows recognition, multiplicity allows self-knowledge and karma is the mechanism through which that recognition matures. In the end, Sanatana Dharma isn't trying to sell you a story, it is inviting you into an inquiry. Not why did God do this to me, but what is this experience teaching consciousness about itself? 

================================================================================

Post Link: https://www.instagram.com/p/DO2YzolCNdW/
Type: Reel
Caption: Meet Salman Habib.

An artist of Indian origin, settled in Spain, living the uncompromising life he has always wanted to.

Salman is funny, carefree, and unapologetically self-centered. For him, his way of living and his freedom rank higher than societal norms and the feelings of other people, including ones that love him. He chose to move away from Rahila and Imran not because of circumstances, but because the domestic life would have been too mundane and suffocating for his hippie-like nature.

He does not harbor any form of suppressed paternal feeling towards his long estranged son, Imran, who was born out of wedlock. Which is why he never bothers (or maybe never cares) to get in touch with Imran, and is okay with having lived a life in which he never met Imran. And which is why he doesn't even approach Imran for a hug when they come face-to-face in the prison.

He nonchalantly rolls a joint as he engages in banter with Imran and his friends, and the first thing he wants to know about Imran is his passion, because for Salman passion comes above it all. And he would have been disappointed had Imran not had any artistic side to him.

Imran at first finds warmth in the realization that his biological father is humorous, witty and artistic like him, because a part of him expects that once his questions are answered he and Salman can start afresh. But as they talk more, Imran realizes that there is no long lost love being reciprocated from the other end. Salman's words show no regret of his past actions, and that pinches Imran. Imran eventually sees too much of himself in Salman, and develops an instant hatred for him.

Salman gives Imran the 'Sorry' that he knows he owes and he knows Imran wants to hear, but not the one he feels, because he does not.

Salman's character is a wonderful catalyst in the story-line, whose choices affected the course of many lives.

--- AUDIO TRANSCRIPT ---
refreshing. Of all the cameos in cinema, Salman Habib is one character that refuses to leave my mind. Written and played with such measured restraint, his four-minute presence feels like a thesis on abandonment, choice and consequence. There's a melodramatic buildup for his character but when we finally meet him, Salman appears disarmingly ordinary. He does not harbour suppressed paternal feelings towards Imran, he in fact seems perfectly fine having lived a life in which he never met Imran, which is why he doesn't even approach him for a hug. Imran at first finds warmth in the realization that his biological father is humorous and creative like him. A part of him hopes that they can start afresh. But as they talk more, Imran realizes that there is no long lost love being reciprocated. Salman is composed to the point of indifference. He does not overcompensate with words or theatrics. His distance may seem cruel but it's a product of a life spent choosing detachment as survival. Salman's silence carries both grace and cowardice. Grace because he wouldn't wound Imran further by pretending and cowardice because it absorbs him from confronting the damage he caused. Salman's eyes have the acknowledgement of Imran's pain but there is no regret in them and in that balance lies the character's brilliance. He is not a villain or a tragic figure, he is just a man who chose a path and learned to live with it. Salman Habib then becomes less a person and more a mirror. His existence asks you what do you owe your children, your family, your lovers? Where does personal freedom end and responsibility begin? And if you lived a life like Salman, would you be able to justify it to yourself with the same stillness?

Post Link: https://www.instagram.com/p/DQNCPI1kzZm/
Type: Reel
Caption: Most people think there’s only one Gita.
The one spoken on a battlefield, between a warrior and a god.
The one that teaches duty, surrender, and devotion.
But there’s another Gita that almost no one talks about.
One that doesn’t teach how to fight or how to pray.
It simply laughs at the entire idea of searching for something.

This Gita doesn’t ask you to become disciplined,
or detached, or awakened.
It doesn’t even tell you to seek God.
Because it starts from the most shocking idea of all,
that there was never anything to seek in the first place.

It says there’s no bondage,
and no liberation.
No seeker,
and no path.
Just awareness, already whole, already free, already untouched by the stories you tell yourself.

It isn’t a book of comfort or moral lessons.
It’s a mirror that shows you the truth your mind keeps running from,
that every effort to find freedom secretly reinforces the illusion that you’re not already free.

Most people won’t understand this text,
and maybe they’re not supposed to.
Because it doesn’t speak to the mind that wants to improve itself.
It speaks to the silence that was there before the mind even began.

It’s called the Avadhuta Gita.
A scripture that doesn’t lead you to enlightenment.
It laughs at the idea that enlightenment was ever missing.

#personaldevelopment #sanatandharma #hindu #spirituality #gita #shiva #stoic #krishna
--------------------
--- AUDIO TRANSCRIPT ---
There is a verse in the Avadhut Gita that most people would find impossible to believe. It says, I have no bondage and no liberation. I have no fear nor the cause of fear. I am consciousness and bliss itself, eternally pure, untouched and whole. That is not some self-help shtick. That is a death sentence to the ego. Because every spiritual path begins with one assumption. That something is wrong with you. That you're caught in ignorance or in karma. And that one day through effort you'll break free. But the Avadhut Gita laughs at that. It says the moment that you start chasing freedom, you've already declared yourself a prisoner. You cannot search for what you already are. The Avadhut literally means the one who has shaken everything off. It doesn't mean a man who renounced society. It means someone who renounced the illusion that there was anything to renounce. He says bondage and liberation are just two sides of the same dream. If you dream of being trapped, you'll also dream of escape. But when you wake up, neither exists. That waking up, that's the real moksha. Modern psychology hints at this but never quite says it. Your mind's sense of self is just a running story that is constantly edited, defended and narrated. The Avadhuta realized the story itself was fiction. He didn't rewrite it, but he stopped believing it instead. It's what Zen calls the gate-less gate. You spend your whole life trying to find the door until you realize that was never a wall. The Stoics said something similar in their own language. Epictetus wrote, You are disturbed not by things but by the views you take of them. The Avadhut would go further. You are disturbed because you think there is a you to disturb. And that is the shock of this text. It's not trying to make you spiritual. It's trying to erase the illusion that spirituality was ever a journey. So the next time your mind says I'm stuck, pause. Don't fight the feeling. Question the identity. Who exactly is stuck? Who is the one that needs saving? If you trace that question far enough, you'll find that there's no prisoner in the cage. Just space. Just awareness. Just you before the story began. The Avadhut Gita ends where most teachings begin. There was never anything to seek because there was never anyone separate from what was sought. You don't become free. You remember. You never weren't.

#personaldevelopment #spirituality #sanatandharma #hindu #vedic #manifestation #cosmos

--- AUDIO TRANSCRIPT ---
Aham Rashtri Samgamani Vasanam. I am speech, the mother of the gods. That is how the Rig Veda begins one of its oldest hymns. Not with creation stories, not with battles, but with a voice declaring its own divinity. Her name is Vak. And the ancients believed that speech itself, not matter, not energy, was the first form of God. The ancients didn't see speech as communication. They saw it as conjuration. Every time you speak, you aim vibration at reality and the world rearranges itself around your sentence. They even worshipped speech as a goddess, because they saw what we've forgotten. That creation begins not with effort, but with articulation. The universe was spoken into existence and so are you. Modern psychology caught up with this thousands of years later. Cognitive science calls it self-authorship. You think in words, you feel in words and you limit yourself in words. Change the words and your reality updates like code. And this truth wasn't only Vedic. The Gospel of John opens with the same revelation. In the beginning was the Word and the Word was with God and the Word was God. Different language, same vision. That creation and consciousness are born from the same source. Sound, meaning, declaration. What the sages called Vag, the mystics of the West called Logos. The divine intelligent that speaks reality into being. That is why truth telling matters, because when you lie, even a little, you bend the geometry of the world around falsehood. Eventually the lie collapses and it buries you under its debris. But when your speech is aligned with reality, with what is, your words cut through chaos like mantras through fog. They become precise, dangerous and alive. In Hindu philosophy they say, the whole cosmos is a vibration known as Vanda. And Vag, the Word, is how consciousness sculpts that vibration into form. So speech isn't an act. It is an act of creation. So think about what you're saying every day about yourself, your goals, your future. Because every careless word is a small spell that you cast carelessly. And every deliberate one is a seed planted in time. You don't need to wait for the universe to listen. It already is.

Post Link: https://www.instagram.com/p/DRg6M1PgHz4/

Caption: They know what’s wrong — but their body won’t let them change.
•
•
#Brain #Psychology #DeepPsychology

--- AUDIO TRANSCRIPT ---
According to psychology, people who see through themselves but still fall back into the same self-destructive patterns are not in denial. They suffer from something called cognitive behavioral dissonance. It means their mind knows exactly what's wrong, but their nervous system hasn't yet learned the safety of allowing something different. They understand everything. They can explain every pattern, name every trauma, and yet they keep doing the same thing simply because their body is still programmed for survival. It's that feeling of, I know this hurts me, but I just don't have the strength to act differently. That's not a lack of awareness, it's emotional exhaustion. It's a nervous system that has learned that stillness feels safer than change. Because when the body has internalized for years that silence means peace, that compliance brings love, and that being small creates safety, then self-healing feels like danger. You see the cage, you even see the open door, but you're too tired to move. And the worst part? Your mind wants to let go, but your body holds on, because repetition feels more familiar than freedom. That's not weakness, that's protection. It's not a mindset problem, it's a survival program that hasn't yet learned that safety can also exist outside of pain. Yes, you know what's wrong, you know what you should do, but knowledge doesn't heal when your body is still living in alert mode. If you see yourself in this, you're not broken, you're tired, and that's a huge difference.

Post Link: https://www.instagram.com/p/DRfRVL5DMhZ/
Type: Reel
Caption: You gave up,
And blamed God.

If you loved someone with your whole chest, you wouldn’t wait years to meet them. You wouldn’t do long-distance with your destiny. You’d cross oceans. You’d tear down walls. You’d fight the world just to stand next to them.

So why do people refuse to fight for the God inside them? Why did comfort become a virtue, and hesitation get dressed up as spirituality?

Somewhere along the way, a whole generation confused passivity with purity. We convinced ourselves that sitting quietly, waiting endlessly, avoiding life and its battles, somehow made us closer to the divine. But it doesn’t work like that. Not in Sanatana Dharma. Not in any tradition that ever produced warriors, saints or seekers.

The truth is uncomfortable. God isn’t found at the end of avoidance. He meets you at the speed you move. Every step you take with awareness becomes yoga. Every effort becomes an offering. Every decision becomes a prayer written in motion.

People think they’ve been abandoned by the divine. But in reality, they abandoned their own fire. They stopped chasing the version of themselves they were meant to become. They stopped acting with the intensity that devotion actually demands.

You won’t meet God by sitting still. You meet Him by living so vividly that every breath becomes tapasya, every action becomes sacred, and every day becomes a step toward the person you’re trying to become.

You didn’t lose God.
You walked away from your own becoming.

Caption: In 1983, physicist Brandon Carter proposed something that made the scientific community go silent: we can’t observe universe-ending catastrophes because if they had happened, we wouldn’t be here to observe them.

But David Deutsch took it further. He proved mathematically that our act of asking “why does the universe exist?” is the universe examining itself.

Consciousness isn’t something the universe contains. Consciousness is how the universe experiences what it’s doing.

A physicist at Oxford ran calculations: if you remove observers from quantum equations, the math describes a universe that never “happens” - it remains in pure probability, never collapsing into reality.

Translation: The universe needs to be witnessed to exist. You’re not watching it - you’re how it watches itself.

Deutsch said something at MIT that made half the room walk out: “You are not atoms experiencing the universe. You are the universe briefly arranged into a form that can experience itself.”

Researchers at Princeton found the complexity for self-awareness is identical to the complexity for the universe to model itself.

Your brain isn’t representing reality - it’s reality creating a localized point of self-reference.

One neuroscientist: “When you observe a star, you’re the universe bending back to look at its own light from 4 billion years ago.”

Deutsch proved every choice you make doesn’t just affect your future - it retroactively determines which past actually happened.

You’re not remembering the past. You’re collapsing it into existence by looking back.

A Cambridge cosmologist said: “If the universe experiences itself through us, then our suffering isn’t happening to us - it’s happening to it.”

The final conclusion: you are not in the universe. You are what the universe is doing right now.

Every thought is the cosmos thinking. Every emotion is existence feeling itself.

Have you noticed that when you understand you’re not separate from this - everything happening *to* you becomes something you’re participating *in*?

Would you call that wisdom or cowardice? The Gita calls it tamas, spiritual laziness, disguised as surrender. You don't escape the movie by walking off the set. You transcend it by playing your role so fully that you no longer confuse yourself with the role. And that is what Krishna calls karma leading to akarma. Action so pure, so free from self-interest that it leaves no residue. And this is the paradox of Sanatana Dharma. You are both the actor and the Avanis watching the play. And only by acting with full intensity can you realize you were never merely the actor. Marcus Aurelius said something similar in the Meditations. Accept everything that happens as if you had chosen it. And that is what it means to align karma and dharma. To move as if destiny itself flows through your hands. And when you say everything is fict, you turn your back on dharma. When you act despite not knowing the outcome, you become its embodiment. So why should you act if everything is already written? Because the script is fixed. But how you perform it isn't. Karma gives you the stage. Dharma decides how you stand upon it. The Enlightened don't run from the movie. They act it out so completely that when the curtain finally falls, there is nothing left but silence. The kind that says the play was perfect.

Post Link: https://www.instagram.com/p/DROrGvrD96F/
Type: Reel
Caption: 1.	In 1965, Rosenthal ran an experiment that many later tried to discredit because the results were inconvenient. Participants were told that certain people in the study were “more gifted,” even though they had been chosen at random. Within eight weeks, those “gifted” participants started acting as if they genuinely were superior, they made decisions faster, doubted themselves less, and their performance increased by almost a quarter.

	2.	Years later, researchers in Zurich repeated the experiment on adults. One group was told they would “perform better” because they supposedly had a “hidden cognitive resource.” The tests were identical to the other group’s, but within 40 minutes their brains began producing answers 89% faster. The researchers said: “We didn’t increase their abilities, we changed their expectations of themselves.”

	3.	One participant admitted he felt a clarity he hadn’t experienced in years: “I just thought I could, and then I could.” Rosenthal insisted there was nothing mystical about it: people act not from their actual abilities, but from what they believe is possible for them. Give someone a version of the future that feels real, and the body begins to behave as if it has already happened.

	4.	The darkest conclusion from the experiment: the Rosenthal effect works in reverse too. If someone is made to believe they are “difficult,” “faulty,” or “a failure,” the brain starts confirming that script, as if it were testing a hypothesis planted in childhood. Neuroscientists have shown that negative expectations from others slow neural transmission almost as much as chronic fatigue.

	5.	At a Harvard conference, a psychologist said a line that made the room go silent:
“We become who others think we are, unless we realize in time that this voice isn’t ours.”
He added that when a person changes not their motivation, but their forecast of themselves, the psyche reorganizes instantly, as if it receives a new operating instruction.

Have you noticed that the biggest turning points in life happen right after you stop believing someone else’s prediction of who you are?

It means you’re not just a body.
Not just your name, your story, your failures.

The source behind every thought.
The stillness behind every storm.

The same fire echoed in other traditions:
Jung’s “Self.”
Taoism’s “The Way.”
Even Stoicism’s inner citadel.
They all point to one realization:
When you remember what you are, you become free.

Free from fear.
Free from shame.
Free from chasing worth in a world built to keep you blind.

If you’ve ever felt like something’s missing…
If you’ve felt disconnected from your power, your center, your truth,
this message is for you.
Sanatana Dharma isn’t just about deities and rituals.
It’s a psychological and spiritual framework to remember your infinite self.
And it starts here, with remembering this truth:
You are Brahman.

Caption: You’re weak.

Most people are. You confuse your reflection for your identity. 

People forget how the Ashtavakra Gita begins.
A young man, bent in eight places, stepped into a court full of kings.
Everyone stared. Some laughed.
But he didn’t flinch. He didn’t apologize for the shape life gave him.
He simply asked why their minds were more twisted than his body.

That’s the part that always gets me.

Because most of us aren’t mocked for our shape, we’re trapped by our self-image.
We break ourselves trying to appear “normal,” trying to fit the expectations of people who aren’t even paying attention.
Meanwhile, a crippled sage stood in front of royalty and taught them freedom.

His teaching wasn’t about leaving the world or renouncing ambition.
It was about not confusing your worth with the mask you wear.
The body breaks, the job shifts, the image fades.
But the one who witnesses it all, the one who watches without collapsing into every emotion or criticism, that’s you.

Every culture has echoed this in its own way.
The Stoics said that no man is free until he conquers himself.
The Samurai trained until fear dissolved and the blade moved without hesitation.
And Ashtavakra said that when you stop clinging to what bends, you finally discover what cannot be bent.

You don’t need a perfect life to be powerful.
You need a clear mind.
You need the courage to stop letting the world tell you what you are.
Because the truth is simple, the parts of you that feel broken are usually the parts that are asking to be seen, not hidden.

So maybe this reel isn’t about a sage with a crooked spine.
Maybe it’s about you.
Maybe it’s the reminder that nothing outside you can define you unless you hand it the permission.

You’re not what twists.

When Ashtavakra walked into King Janaka's court, everyone laughed at him. His body was bent in eight places, twisted, mishappened and mocked by the world. But he just smiled and said, you laugh at my body, but I don't even live in it. And that is how the Ashtavakra Gita begins. A crippled sage standing in a hall of kings, teaching them what freedom really means. There's a verse where he says that you're not the body, nor the senses, nor the mind. You are the witness, the consciousness that watches over. Most people, lazy people will read that and think it's about giving up the world. But it's not about escape. It is about mastering the world that you're living in. Because when you stop mistaking yourself for the mask that you wear, no failure can humiliate you and no victory can possess you. And that is not some spiritual fluff. That is actually a very sound strategy. The Stoics call this Apothea, unshakable stillness in chaos. Epictetus who was a slave who said, no man is free until he conquers himself. Ashtavakra said the same thing centuries earlier. That freedom isn't a reward, it is your nature. In Japan, the samurai trained for years to reach Mushin, the state of no mind. The sportsman who stops identifying with fear becomes unstoppable. When the blade moves without the burden of thought, perfection happens on its own. And in the West, Nietzsche wrote about the Yuba-Mensch, a man who creates his own values untouched by the herd. But Ashtavakra went even further. He dissolved the herd, the self and even the creator. He said the one who sees beyond all roles isn't above the world, he is the world. Awake within it. Think about it. A man twisted in eight places taught the most beautiful truth that you are not what bends, you are what remains when everything bends. In today's world, people break because they think that their reflection defines them. Their looks, their job, their image, their mistakes. But Ashtavakra didn't need validation. He didn't need to fix the mirror he saw through it. And that is the kind of strength that built civilizations. The warriors calm before the battle, the philosophers peace in exile, the entrepreneurs clarity when everyone doubts him. Ashtavakra's lesson is not to run from the world, but to play your part without letting the part consume you. Because the real victory isn't in conquering others. It is in remembering who you were before you became someone. So the next time you feel bent, broken or misunderstood, remember the sage who laughed at his own chains. He wasn't mocking the world, he was freeing it. And maybe that is what you need to do too. Stop trying to fix your reflection and start remembering the light that's looking through it.

In the Padma Puran and Skandapuran, there is a line that most people read and move on from. There are 8.4 million species of life through which the soul must wander before attaining the human form. 8.4 million lives. That is how many forms you've worn before becoming what you call you. It says 900,000 species of aquatic life, 2 million of plants and trees, 1.1 million of insects, 1 million of birds, 3 million of beasts and 400,000 of human forms. That isn't just some random list. It is a map, an ancient metaphysical blueprint of evolution. Long before Darwin spoke of natural selection, the rishis were speaking of spiritual progression. They didn't see evolution as chance. They saw it as consciousness slowly remembering itself. In Sanatana Dharma, life doesn't begin, it continues. Every form is a lesson. Every birth is a classroom. And each one refines the soul bit by bit until it becomes aware enough to ask, who am I? And that is what the sages called Jeeva Yatra, the pilgrimage of consciousness through form. The human birth isn't the start of life. It is the moment life becomes self-aware. And that is why the Bhagavad Puran says that after many many births, the human form is attained. It is most rare and valuable. Because only now as a human you can transcend instinct and choose dharma. Only now you can rise above the mechanical dance of karma and become the conscious creator of your destiny. You see, a lion cannot choose compassion. A river cannot choose restraint. But you can. And that is what makes you sacred. The Manusmriti even calls this body the Deha Yajna Padra. The vessel through which sacrifice and self-realization are possible. That is why it is said that even the gods envy human birth. Because only from here you can reach what even they cannot. Moksha. Rishis said, Nayam hanti nahanyate. The self neither kills nor is killed. The body dissolved but the pattern continues. Learning, evolving and returning. So the 8.4 million lives are not superstition, they are symbolism. A way to remind you that your existence isn't random. It's recursive. The universe learning itself through you. And that is the mystery of these 8.4 million lives. Every animal you fear, every instinct you suppress, you've been that. Every impulse, from hunger to hatred to hope. It is the residue of lives you've already lived. Philosophers like Nietzsche said that man is a rope stretched between beast and god. The Rishis said the same thing thousands of years earlier. Only they added the rest of the truth. That rope isn't stretched, it is alive. It is moving, it is remembering. And that is why the human birth is called Mukti Dvar, the doorway to liberation. Because only here, in this fragile body of clay and consciousness, you can stop running the endless race of rebirth and finally turn around toward the one who was never running. Maybe this life isn't your first chance. But it might be your turning point. Because 8.4 million lives brought you here. Not to escape the world, but to become conscious enough to love it, heal it, and rise through it.

in Tibetan monasteries for centuries because the monks understood its power. They knew that breath, used correctly, is the fastest way to alter consciousness and reshape reality. Carbon dioxide is building slightly, and this specific combination triggers a cascade of neurochemical changes. Your vagus nerve is activating. BDNF is beginning to release. Stay relaxed during the hold. No tension in your face, neck, or shoulders. The exhale, five seconds. Open your mouth slightly and release the breath slowly over five seconds. As you exhale, drop your jaw, relax your shoulders, and let go of any tension you're holding. This exhale should feel like a release. Imagine you're letting go of stress, old patterns, pain. The exhale is actually more important than the inhale for nervous system regulation. This is when your parasympathetic nervous system fully engages.

There's a famous Victor Frankl quote, when a man can't find a deep sense of meaning, they distract themselves with pleasure. Frankl is arguing that a lack of meaning causes people to seek temporary relief in superficial pursuits, rather than addressing the underlying existential void. Caption: Money is sin.
That’s what most people have been taught, even if they don’t say it out loud.

We grew up hearing that wanting more makes you greedy, that ambition is unspiritual, that the purest life is the quietest one.
And so, people started hiding behind the idea of detachment to justify their fear of failure.
They called it surrender. They called it faith.
But in truth, it was comfort wearing the mask of spirituality.

The ancients never saw it that way.
In the Manusmriti, life was built on four pillars — Dharma, Artha, Kama, and Moksha.
Not one, not two, but four.
Because the sages knew that you can’t walk toward truth if your foundation is broken.
Without Artha, even Dharma falls apart.
You can’t serve others when you’re dependent on them.
You can’t preach truth when your life is ruled by scarcity.

Somewhere along the way, people forgot that prosperity isn’t greed.
It’s the ability to give, to build, to create, to protect.
That’s why Bhishma told Yudhishthira that Artha is the ground on which Dharma stands.
Without it, even virtue becomes fragile.

But modern spirituality twisted that wisdom.
It turned strength into arrogance, and weakness into holiness.
People started glorifying passivity, calling ambition “ego” and discipline “obsession.”
Yet the Rishis never condemned wealth.
They condemned attachment to it.
They knew that money in the hands of a wise person is like fire, it can destroy, but it can also cook, protect, and create warmth.

The greatest sages, kings, and thinkers weren’t ashamed of power.
They mastered it.
Because power doesn’t corrupt everyone.
It only exposes who you already are.

It was about shaping it.

#personaldevelopment #sanatandharma #hindu #spirituality #krishna #shiva #dharma #india
--------------------
--- AUDIO TRANSCRIPT ---
In the Manusmriti it is written Dharm, Arth, Kaam and Moksha. These four are the aims of life. But somewhere along the way we decided only the last one mattered. We turned spirituality into escapism and forgot that the ancient sages never separated heaven from earth. Arth, the pursuit of wealth, success and stability wasn't seen as greed. It was seen as responsibility. Because without Arth even Dharm collapses. You cannot serve your family, your society or your purpose if you're broke, dependent or undisciplined. In the Mahabharata, Bhishma tells Yudhishthira that Arth is like the foundation of a house. Dharma is the walls and Moksha is the roof. But without wealth, without structure, the rest cannot stand. And somewhere along the way, India's as well as the world's spiritual message got twisted. We started glorifying poverty and condemning ambition. But the rishis were never against prosperity. They were against attachment. They knew that wealth in the hands of the wise will uplift, while in the hands of the ignorant it will enslave. And that is why in the Arthashastra, Chanakya calls Arth the root of all human activity. Even Dharm and Moksha, he says, depend upon it. He wasn't worshipping gold. He was warning us that without material strength even truth has no protection. Compare that with the stoics. They never rejected wealth either. Marcus Aurelius ruled an empire and still wrote about peace. Seneca was one of the richest men in Rome and still spoke of virtue. Because virtue doesn't mean poverty. It means power under control. So the real sanat and path wasn't world denying. It was world mastering. Arth was never anti-spiritual. It was the ground on which spirituality walked. You're not meant to escape the world. You're meant to refine it, organize it and beautify it. To turn chaos into order and wealth into offering. The ancients understood something that we've forgotten. That money is energy and energy needs direction. Without Dharm it corrupts and without Arth it decays. And you need both. So maybe spirituality isn't just about renouncing your desires. Maybe it is about training them. Maybe Arth isn't the enemy of enlightenment. Maybe it is the path to sustain it. Because the highest Dharm isn't starving on the road to Moksha. It is walking it with strength, stability and grace.

Because everything else in business can be learned. You can learn new skills, you can build contacts, you can try new business models, but no one's going to come and install this mindset of controlled optimism in you. Where you learn to keep going even when there's a foggy road ahead with no guarantees. It's easy to be optimistic when you're winning. For example, it was much easier for me to start Smash Guys this year because I already had evidence that we could build successful brands. First with the pizza bakery and then with Paris Panini. But that first outlet of the pizza bakery that we started in 2017 required more conviction than all our subsequent outlets combined. Because when you're a nobody, with no validation, no data, no guarantees, that optimism becomes your fuel. People usually believe that conviction comes from past results because a proven track record of having achieved something gives you confidence when you chase a new idea. And that's true. But when you're starting out, it's the other way around. Your results come from your conviction and the willingness to bet on that conviction with controlled optimism. For this trait, you won't last long enough to experience your first success. Stay tuned for episode 6.

For example, if the gravitational constant were just a hair bigger or smaller, the stars of the early universe would be too hot or too cool to produce any life essential elements. If the electromagnetic force were just a hair stronger or weaker, atoms could form into molecules and the entire universe would be a giant cloud of subatomic particles. If the mass of a tiny little proton was just a tiny bit bigger, there'd be no hydrogen, which means no hydrogen stars, which means no carbon and oxygen made in those stars, which basically means no water and no biology. So it appears, to most physicists not all, that since the very beginning, the entire universe has been perfectly tuned for one thing. Now physicists admit they have no idea how these numbers came to be so perfectly and they all agree it would be unreasonable to think that it all just happened by chance. So according to, there are only two possible explanations. Either these numbers were set by a super intellect or they occurred naturally in one universe amidst trillions upon trillions upon trillions upon trillions of other universes that we could never possibly know exist. Now there's not a shred of scientific evidence to support either of these, so both require a giant.

--- AUDIO TRANSCRIPT ---
The second principle might sound contradictory at first. I call it engaged detachment. To be fully committed to the present action while remaining completely unconcerned with its outcome. In 1967, I participated in a karate tournament in Long Beach. Before my match, I observed my opponent pacing, rehearsing moves, visualizing victory. His desire to win created tension in his body. Narrowed his vision. When we faced each other, something unusual happened. As he attacked with intense determination to defeat me, I found myself in a state I hadn't planned. Completely present, yet strangely detached. I was fully engaged in each movement, every block, every strike executed with total commitment. Yet simultaneously I held no attachment to winning or losing. This paradoxical state allowed me to see openings invisible to him. To respond without the delay of thought or the hesitation of fear. This principle applies far beyond combat. Consider how attachment to outcomes affects your daily life. When you desperately need a particular result, your body tenses, your mind narrows, your breathing becomes shallow. You become blind to alternatives. Deaf to subtle information. Many believe detachment means not caring, being passive or indifferent. This misses the point entirely. True detachment isn't about caring less. It's about clinging less. You can care deeply about what you do while releasing your grip on how things must turn out. In my film work, I discovered this same principle. The scenes that worked best weren't when I focused on impressing the audience, but when I immersed myself completely in the moment with no concern for how I would be perceived. Total engagement with the action, total detachment from its reception. Here's a practice I developed. Before any important event, I ask myself, can I give everything to this moment while holding nothing about its outcome? This shifts my energy from the future, which doesn't exist, to the present, which is the only place power resides. Engaged detachment creates a curious freedom. When you're no longer constrained by the need for things to unfold in a specific way, you discover an expansive power to respond to what actually is. Your mind becomes like a skilled sailor who works with whatever wind arises.
